## Critiques of corporate power and influence

Writing from less design-oriented perspectives many other writers8 have presented a different view of the power of the multinational corporation to influence social, economic, and political life. As the American economist J. K. Galbraith argued in his introduction to the 1974 British edition of his widely-read book, The New Industrial State:

It is central to my case that power in modern industrial society resides with the large producing organisations—the corporations. So, far from being safely and resignedly subordinate to the market, as the neo-classical argument holds, they fix prices, and go on extensively to accommodate the consumer to their needs. And they also obtain from the state such further action as is needed to ensure a benign and stable environment for their operations.

This, Galbraith argued, was brought about through the economic power of the large-scale corporation to compete with the state in terms of planning and influence. In 1968, General Motors had a gross revenue of \$22.8 billion, equivalent to about one-eighth of the total receipts of Federal Government in the United States. It accorded with Galbraith's model of large-scale American capitalism in its ability, through sheer size, to influence government, control markets, and dictate prices and wage settlements for the whole automobile industry (only one element of its industrial activities), as well as establishing patterns of consumer taste.

Cover of Time magazine showing Coca-Cola as the 'World's Friend', 15 May 1950 This celebrated illustration confirmed the way in which Coca-Cola had become a truly global product by the midcentury, much stimulated by its popularity amongst American troops stationed across the world during the Second World War and its aftermath. It came to be readily associated with the material values and desires of American consumer society, a symbol of material affluence.

Such critiques often assumed a more overt political dimension with the global marketing of products which were increasingly identified with a particular way of life. This assumed particular strength in the immediate post-1945 period when products such as Coca-Cola [102] and Levi's jeans became emblems of a 'democratic', essentially American, lifestyle in countries such as Japan [103] and Germany which were seeking to throw off their recent associations with oppressive political regimes. This process has intensified in the post-Berlin Wall era of the 199os with the proliferation in the former Eastern bloc of McDonald's restaurants and other outlets and products identified with the overt values of consumerism and at least symbolic 'freedom of choice'.

## 102

Critiques of what has been termed American 'cultural imperialism' were often highly politicized, as in Ariel Dorfman and Armand

![](images/fc5882e301d60cca1712a3de5fc8ae317703d21e02c6f0eeb191e5941ca77671.jpg)

Levi's advertisement, Japan, early 1980s: 'Since 1850 Jeans Have Been Called Levi's in the USA'

Although jeans originated in the mid-nineteenth century as Californian workers' clothing made by Levi Strauss they have since become potent and expressive emblems of widely differing values throughout the twentieth century. Associated with teenage rebellion in the 1950s on the one hand, or expensive 'Designer' brand names in the late 1970s and early 1980s on the other, their changing meanings paralleled those of the T-shirt which successfully made the transition from working vest to emblematic carrier of slogans and images.

![](images/3277c00603e4e6560e098097105e89d5fc2732f3faf2e99bccd670f08faa1f26.jpg)

Mattelart's controversial text of 1971 Para Leer al Pato Donald (How to Read Donald Duck),1 which was written in the volatile climate of the Chilean revolution. An ideology associated with the consumerist nirvana of wealth and leisure, as epitomized by the extensive range of American comics, films, and television programmes which sustained such an outlook, was denounced by the authors as irrelevant to the real needs of under-developed countries, particularly Chile, which they saw as having become fundamentally dependent, in economic and cultural terms, on the United States.

The highly visible presence internationally of logotypes and branding associated with specific products and companies has been a powerful testimony to the perceived ability of design to communicate particular cultural values, as well as to engender corporate profitability. In a later text Mattelart also drew attention to widespread perceptions that multinational corporations, and their products, were seen increasingly as threats to national sovereignty. However, there was evidence that this was recognized by business itself:

.in April 1975, the President of the large North American publicity agency Doyle-Dane-Bernbach11 asserted before students of a Canadian University that 'Multinational corporations could solve a part of their image problem through the establishment of global universities'. And, he added, going further into the description of his project, that by adopting this formula companies such as Exxon, Texaco or General Electric would have much less need for support by Government, trades union circles and consumers from accusations made against them of violating national sovereignty. The proposal is very simple: the companies would open a university course of two years for students from both industrialised and underdeveloped countries, with the requirement only 'that they spoke perfect English'. These courses could deal with the history of the company, the analysis of concrete examples of marketing, management, international law, the relationship of the enterprise with the community, its social responsibility. Provided with a diploma granted by the company, the students would return to their respective countries and would constitute a kind of common denominator of understanding' around the world.12

## Early manifestations of corporate identity: a historical overview

Although corporate identity has assumed an increasingly high profile in the post-1945 era, it has manifested itself over a far longer period through the formation, organization, and visual projection of groups as varied as armies,13 political regimes,14 and railway companies. In Britain, for example, the great railway companies of the nineteenth century made concerted attempts to project their own individual identities across many aspects of their visible presence, from railway termini through to coach livery, uniforms, and dining-car tableware. Design historians have laid particular emphasis on the corporate identity work of designers such as Peter Behrens at the Allgemeine Elektrizitäts-Gesellchaft in the early years of the twentieth century in Germany, as discussed in Chapter 1. Significant examples of corporate identity policies can be found in many other countries, as in Britain in the buildings, locomotives, rolling stock, buses, signage, advertising, fittings, and fabrics of the London Passenger Transport Board under the operational direction of Frank Pick in the 193os, or in the United States with Walter Dorwin Teague's sriking designs for Texaco petrol stations.

Other notable examples prior to the Second World War include the Olivetti offce equipment company in Italy, where a corporate identity policy emerged in the wake of Adriano Olivetti's initiatives in modernizing the firm's business and production methods in the later 1920s. Much of the impetus had derived from a visit to the United States in 1926, and an advertising office was established in 1928. Following his appointment as general manager in 1933, a wide range of initiatives were adopted in the projection of the public, and also internal, identity of the company, sustained by the employment of a number of leading modernist architects and designers who worked on all aspects of the company's visible appearance, from brochures, advertising and office machines through to factory buildings and workers' housing. A Bauhaus graduate, Alexander Schawinsky, worked on graphic and product design from 1933 to 1936; Marcello Nizzoli, a renowned exhibition and graphic designer, became the company's chief consultant in 1936; in the same year Giovanni Pintori also joined the company and, like Nizzoli, added to Olivett's reputation through publicity, product, and architectural design. This very much set the scene for the further development of an enlightened corporate policy in the post-Second

World War era with the employment of designers of the stature of Ettore Sottsass Jr. and Mario Bellini and the increasingly significant role given to cultural projection. The company's commitment to corporate identity design was recognized in 1974 when the American Institute of Architects awarded Olivetti the Industrial Arts Medal 'for a history of excellence in communicating its image through product design, corporate communications, architecturally distinguished manufacturing and merchandising facilities, and sponsorship of numerous social, educational, recreational, and cultural programs for both its employees and the public at large'.

In the United States corporate identity design also assumed increasing importance in the interwar years. The Container Corporation of America (CCA) was an important pioneer in this respect in the 193os, largely through the enlightened patronage of the company's founder, the Chicago industrialist Walter P. Paepke. In 1936, wishing to communicate the company's commitment to a modern outlook and product quality, he employed the N. W. Ayer advertising agency as a means of bringing this about. In the same year he also appointed Egbert Jacobsen as Director of the company's new Design Department. Amongst those designers commissioned to produce advertisements were the French designers A. M. Cassandre and Fernand Léger, the former Bauhaus tutors Lázló Moholy-Nagy and Herbert Bayer, Herbert Matter, the Swiss emigré designer and poster artist, and the Hungarian-born graphic designer, Gyorgy Kepes. Combinations of photomontage with typography and other techniques associated with the European avant-garde were incorporated into company publicity, strongly influencing the outlook of other designers working for the company. Such strong links with European modernism were sustained by the establishment, in 1937, of the New Bauhaus in Chicago under Lázló Moholy-Nagy. Later becoming the School of Design, then School of Design in Chicago, and finally the Institute of Design,15 a number of former Bauhaus graduates and masters were employed on the staff.

In the post-Second World War period Walter Paepke, Egbert Jacobsen, and Herbert Bayer were at the forefront of moves which sought to further understanding in business circles of the significant economic potential of design in industry. In 1951 Paepke inaugurated the first Design Conference at the Aspen Institute of Humanistic Studies in Colorado.16 Initiating debate about the wider social, cultural, and philosophical implications of design it sought to bring together leading figures from design, industry, government, and education. At the 1951 meeting both Olivetti and the Container Corporation of America were cited as exemplars of the implementation of a consistent design policy in industry. Held annually, in 1954 it became incorporated as the International Design Conference in

Aspen (IDCA) and was soon established as a major forum for design debate, with contributions from leading designers, intellectuals, and industrialists from all over the world.

## Design and the multinational corporation after 1945

As has been discusse in Chapter 4, theaspirations of those seeking t implement the modernist creed in the interwar years had been severely blunted by the rising tide of national identity in the increasingly difficult political and economic circumstances of the times. However, after the Second World War, with the widespread discrediting of totalitarian regimes and the waning of the power of imperialism in the Western industrialized world, the internationalizing values inherent in modernism no longer carried the same threat to national identity. With the postwar reorientation of traditional trading patterns and the need to develop new outlets many large-scale companies viewed the penetration of global markets as an increasingly important goal. As part of this strategy multinational corporations assumed the mantle of modernism, their international presence marked by efficient-looking buildings, products, and co-ordinated corporate design programmes.

As has been suggested earlier, the Olivetti company epitomized high standards for corporate identity design and implementation. It was particularly influential on the outlook of International Business Machines (IBM), a company which emerged as a world leader in the field of office equipment after the end of the Second World War. IBM's distinctive corporate style, which embraced everything from brochures and sign systems to dictating machines and company buildings, emerged after the appointment of the architect-designer Eliot Noyes as Consultant Director of Design in 1956 [104]. He had first worked on the IBM account in Norman Bel Geddes's office ten years earlier, continuing to work on a company retainer after setting up his own design practice in 1947. He brought in Paul Rand as Graphics Coordinator as well as a number of other design consultants including Charles Eames, George Nelson, and Edgar Kaufmann Jr. They were all heavily influenced by European modernism: Noyes had worked with the former Bauhaus masters Walter Gropius and Marcel Breuer at Harvard in the 193os, and was Director of the Department of Industrial Design at the Museum of Modern Art (MOMA) in New York from 1940-2 and 1945-6; Paul Rand was well aware of European modernism in the interwar years, an interest stimulated by magazines such as the German Gebrauchsgraphik17 and Advertising Arts; George Nelson had studied in Rome in the 193os and, with the designer Henry Wright, co-edited Architectural Forum, a magazine which strongly promoted the modernist cause and frequently featured many aspects of corporate design; after a brief interregnum by Suzanne Wasson-Tucker, Edgar Kaufmann Jr. had succeeded Noyes as Director of the

## 104 Eliot Noyes

Selectric typewriter, IBM, 1961 Noyes, who had worked for Gropius and Breuer in the 1930s, had also been director of industrial design at MOMA, New York. He was brought in as design consultant to IBM in 1956 by the corporation's President, Thomas Watson, and soon gave the company a modern, international corporate image. This pervaded its buildings, graphic communications, and products, much in the way that previously the image for the Olivetti company had done. This particular typewriter also introduced the 'golfball' as a replacement for type-bars.

![](images/eec5b3d8d43b1d0ee014c2c85981b4b5de2eec25021dcc5962b39a0cdca316cd.jpg)

Department of Industrial Design at MOMA, a strong advocate of modernism and promoter of the annual Good Design Exhibitions held at the Museum between 1950 and 1955. Marcel Breuer and Eero Saarinen, an American architect-designer with Scandinavian roots, were also commissioned to design buildings for IBM.

The IBM design outlook was enshrined in its Design Practice Manual, written by Noyes. The company soon became renowned for a corporate identity which symbolized advanced technology and organizational efficiency through its crisp graphics (as in Rand's logotype of 1956, see 105), the neat, clean lines of such office products as the 1961 Selectric typewriter, and the efficient, functional, yet pleasant, ethos of its architecture and interiors. Such an outlook (also promoted by Olivetti and other companies) additionally endowed the office environment with notions of status and aesthetic sensitivity and did much to help persuade potential employees that clerical work was an attracivativ motiv wa l factory work in the 195os and 196os.18 IBM's policy of design coordination throughout the corporation was continued in the following decades, resulting in official recognition from the American design profession: in 198o IBM was awarded both the Design Leadership Award by the American Institute of Graphic Arts and an award for long-term excellence in design by the Industrial Designers of America (IDSA).

Other large American companies were also significant exponents of corporate identity in the decades following the Second World War. By the mid-195os the Westinghouse Electric Corporation had become increasingly concerned with its design image, with frequent complaints by retailers that the company was not oriented clearly enough towards the consumer and could not keep up with its competitors in terms of product development. Its position was further affected by a

Corporate design was an important part of the armoury of national and multinational corporations in the post-Second World War period. These particular symbols and their designers are celebrated in their field.

series of strikes and the radical dropping of prices in 1956 by General Electric, the market leader in consumer appliances. Following detailed analysis of the position, Eliot Noyes was brought in to Westinghouse in 196o in order to improve standards. In turn he brought in Paul Rand, who produced a striking logotype, based on electric circuitry and the letter W. Noyes's other consultancies have included Mobil (1964) and Pan-Am (1969-72), whilst Rand has also produced logos for ABC Television and United Parcels Service.

The interiors of many large corporations were also endowed with an internationalizing modernist aesthetic, sustained by the furniture of companies such as Herman Miller and Knoll. Although Herman Miller had begun to explore a modernist outlook in the 193os through the enlightened appointment of Gilbert Rohde as the company's designer, it was not until the appointment of George Nelson as director of design in 1944 that the company began to orient itself towards the architectural, and thus largely corporate, market. Nelson also brought in Charles Eames as a consultant to the company, reinforcing the modernist ethos in furniture production. There was considerable activity in the field of office furniture, most notably the Action Office, which was developed from the late 195os by Nelson and Robert Propst, and marketed in its first version from 1964.

Knoll Associates, founded by the German-born Hans Knoll and his wife Florence in New York in 1946, also did much to support the modernist corporate environment through its furniture and furnishings, which could be found in many of the impressive foyers of large corporations [106]. Florence had studied at the Cranbrook Academy of Art and at the Illinois Institute of Technology, both institutions with strong European affiliations. She also knew Mies van der Rohe and worked with Gropius and Breuer. Building on such links, the Knolls marketed designs by leading Europeans, many of them being seen as 'classics' of modern design, especially those by Breuer, Saarinen, and Mies, who granted the company the production rights of his designs of the 1920s.

Other companies gained considerable recognition for their identity in the same period. In Germany, for example, the Braun company set high standards of a coherent rational design aesthetic with the introduction of Otl Aicher and Hans Gugelot from the pioneering Hochschule für Gestaltung at Ulm alongside the industrial designer, Dieter Rams, in the mid-195os. In 1956 the company's design department was established, coming under Rams's direction in 1960. During his lengthy association with the company a clear identity emerged, characterized by the white, sculptural form of the KM2 Kitchen Machine designed by G.A. Müller in 1957 [107] or the HLD 4 Hairdryer designed by Rams in 197o. The austere, efficient lines of the company's domestic appliances were complemented by the rational

It should be considered, in passing, that a coherent design programme did not in itself ensure market success: American Gillette took Braun over, and further diffculties were experienced in the competitive market-place of the early 1990s.

The work of a wide range of influential corporate identity consultancies such as Chermayeff & Geismar Inc. of New York, Pentagram of London or Total Design of Holland might be enumerated in this context, along with many instances of a multinational corporate design policy, such as those adopted by Philips,19 Coca-Cola,2 or Sony.21 However, there are other mechanisms worthy of attention which have done much to support the globalization of specific products or cultural values, many of them enshrined in the corporate ethos of companies already discussed.

## The role of museums and exhibitions in promoting a global sesign culture

While a growing number of museums are beginning to embrace wider definitions of design culture in the twentieth century, the majority have endorsed a policy which is centred on celebrated products or the output of specific designers whose work is seen to embrace high standards of aesthetic distinction or cultural status. The twentieth-century design galleries of many museums around the world have their roots in the collection and display of objects which, it was originally felt, would 'improve' standards of taste and enhance the cultural well-being of their visitors. Furthermore, those charged with selection policies and the acquisition of material were generally drawn from a narrow social and cultural spectrum and, when they were bold enough to step outside their own national conventions of artistic excellence, often favoured objects which conformed to the modernist canon. The meanings of such clean, abstract forms, stripped of superfluous ornamentation and symbolically compatible with the modern machine-age production technology, have changed alongside the reshaping of cultural and political perspectives both before and after the Second World War, as has been discussed in Chapter 2 (and will be examined further in Chapter 8). As has been argued, modernist products became increasingly associated with notions of 'Good Design', opposed both to retrospective indulgence in historicizing ornament in domestic products on the one hand and to the science-fiction-inspired futuristic chrome fantasies and tail-fins of automobiles on the other. Modernist products were also seen as oppositional to notions of built-in obsolescence and ephemerality and thus, particularly when placed in a spotlit, pedestalized museum—rather than a social—context, assumed something of the status of 'design classic'.

## Modernism and MOMA, New York

MOMA, founded in New York in 1929 'to encourage and develop the study of the modern arts and the application of such arts to manufacture and public life', did much to establish such an outlook. Its Department of Architecture and Industrial Art was established in 1932

## 107 Gerd Aifred Muller and Robert Oberheim

Kitchen Machine KM 32, Braun, 1957

By the mid-1950s Braun emerged as a company with high aesthetic principles in product design, having forged links with the nationalist Hochschule für Gestaltung at Ulm. A design department was established at the company in 1956, the KM3 of the following year epitomizing the geometric clarity which was to be associated with Braun products for many years, particularly after 1960 when Dieter Rams became Braun's chief designer. A permanent display of Braun products was put on display at MOMA, New York, in 1958.

![](images/68885ac5f31d3ec023c48290b3ffd76825ed0828354278e8aa0e0c6333a2a45e.jpg)

and mounted a series of didactic design exhibitions promoting modernism, commencing with the Machine Art show of 1934 which was curated by Philip Johnston. It was in the same year that MOMA's Design Collection was formally inaugurated, commencing with a selection from the Machine Art exhibition [12]. Two criteria were identified for the selection of objects—'quality' and 'historical' significance:

An object is chosen for its quality because it is thought to achieve, or have originated, those formal ideals of beauty which have become the major style concepts of our time.

Historical significance is a more flexible criterion. It applies to objects which may not resolve problems of aesthetics and function with total success, but which nevertheless have contributed importantly—or may yet contribute—to the development of design.22

In 1939 the Museum held a ten-year retrospective exhibition in which industrial design played a comparatively minor role. MOMA's stance against the ephemeral styling of American products was revealed by the inclusion of only one American designer, Richard Buckminster Fuller, a fierce critic of contemporary American industrial design. The more favoured European and Scandinavian modernism was represented through chairs by Breuer, Mies Van Der Rohe, Le Corbusier, and Alvar Aalto. This design creed was continued by Eliot Noyes and Edgar Kaufmann Jr., in charge of MOMA's Department of Industrial Design in the 1940s and 195os. Kaufmann also mounted a series of Good Design exhibitions between 1950 and 1955 [108, cf. 109], the precepts for which were largely enumerated in his definitive text, What Is

## 108

'Good Design Show' organized by Edgar Kaufmann Jr. for MOMA, NY, 1951 Kaufmann's 'Good Design' programme was underpinned by a collaboration between MOMA, New York, and The Merchandise Mart, Chicago, representing respectively the interests of art and commerce. It was aimed at museum audiences and the wholesale buyers who represented the retail trade, seeking to educate both the public and manufacturing industry. The pronounced emphasis on form rather than decoration seen in this installation by Finn Juhl reflected the modernist credo followed by Kaufmann.

![](images/b8e1534ab5149ac5858cbddb1b2ead20ec83aa6dc15609cf25797a4e4ceec55f.jpg)

Interior of the Vitra Design Museum at Weil am Rhein, 1989

This innovative museum was built largely as a gallery for the chair collection of Rolf Fehlbaum of the Vitra furniture manufacturing company. Pedestalized and removed from their primary function, they are to be viewed as art and symbols of aesthetic status.

Modern Design? of 195o.23 Consolidating such a morally-charged approach to design, the exhibition 'Olivetti: Design in Industry' was put on in 1952, the first time a European manufacturer had been invited to display its products and graphic design at the Museum. Coming two years after the establishment of the Olivetti Corporation of America in New York, the show exemplified what was felt to be good practice, a practice which the company had itself done much to validate through external promotion. As was written at the time in MOMA's Bulletin,

The Olivetti company, many critics agree, is the leading corporation in the western world in the field of design. For patronage in architecture, product design and advertising, it would only be difficult to name a second . ., It is not only the aesthetic quality of these achievements that is remarkable. More important as a lesson in our industrial world of today is the organisation of all the visual aspects of an industry, unified under a single high standard of taste.

The postwar inheritance of a form of European modernism was further consolidated when Braun products were put on permanent display at MOMA from 1958, following growing approbation of the German company's new design policy at the 1957 Milan Triennale, the Frankfurt Radio and Television Exposition, and the Berlin

![](images/6bda74bbd24b7fc32cc01becb4c75a2886165f481433d1865f0aad42fb4a8fd8.jpg)

Publicity for Scenicruiser Greyhound bus, designed by Raymond Loewy Associates, 1954

Loewy devoted considerable attention to new transportation designs, endowing them with style and panache. He had worked with the Greyhound company since 1940, when he designed the Silversides motor coach. It was replaced by the sleek, streamlined, and obviously American Scenicruiser, and its high standards of passenger comfort, together with the development of the US highway system, were intended to attract inter-city travellers in the face of competition from rail and the air.

International Building Exhibition, where its products were seen in the majority of show houses.

There was a similar institutionalization of the notion of Good Design in Europe, often with support from the State, as with the Council of Industrial Design (COD), established in Britain in 1944 or the Rat für Formgebung (Design Council) in Germany, which, following a 1951 mandate in the German Parliament, was inaugurated in 1953. The COID was charged from the outset with 'improving' standards of design in the consumer and manufacturing sectors and initiated a Good Design Award Scheme for selected products in 1957, the year after opening its own Design Centre in the Haymarket, London. The awards generally recognized products which conformed to the modernist canon—genteel and restrained, and devoid of excessive ornamentation or ephemeral styling. Similar awards reflecting parallel aesthetic priorities were offered in other European countries, including the Compasso d'Oro scheme, initiated by La Rinascente department store in Italy in 1954, and the Beauté France award, made through the French Ministry of Commerce.

That there was an established international canon of 'taste' in the c   J

Doblin, Director of the Institute of Design at the Illinois Institute of Technology, in 1958.25 He wrote to 100 of the 'leading' designers, architects, and design teachers around the world, asking them to identify the ten 'best designed mass-produced products of modern times'. About 8o ballots were returned and, after the issue of further questionnaires, a final order was drawn up. Although the overall ranking was significantly more accommodating with regard to ephemeral styling than would have been the case with MOMA, the OID, and comparable organizations with a commitment to the concept of Good Design, the top three products were the Olivetti Lettera 22 portable typewriter of 195o, designed by Marcello Nizzoli, the Eames side-chair of 1947, produced by Herman Miller, and Mies Van Der Rohe's Barcelona Chair, originally designed in 1929, produced by Knoll Associates. Raymond Loewy Associates-designed products were featured six times (including the Coldspot refrigerator for Sears Roebuck, a 1948 vacuum cleaner for Singer, and the 1954 Scenicruiser greyhound bus [110]), but overall the favoured products generally looked to modernism, with a particular leaning towards designs from Europe and Scandinavia, including designs by Aalto, Barnack, Breuer, D'Ascanio, Ghia, Gretsch, Nizzoli, Pinin Farina, Ponti, Porsche, Thonet Brothers, and Wegner.

## The corporation and cultural heritage

As has been seen in the case of Herman Miller and Knoll International in the United States, a significant number of companies were active in the promotion of the international style through the marketing of high-quality furniture with a heavy premium on aesthetic distinction. Cassina, the Milan-based furniture company, played a similar role, moving into the field of architect-designed furniture from the late 1940s onwards, with a particular emphasis on Italian designers, such as Franco Albini, Gio Ponti, Vico Magistretti, and Mario Bellini. From 1965 it also initiated a policy of producing a range of past classics from the late nineteenth and earlier twentieth centuries. Entitled the 'Masters' series, it commenced with manufacture, to the original design, of the 1929 chaise longue by Le Corbusier, Charlotte Perriand, and Pierre Jeanneret. Later, particularly in the heritage-aware 1980s when the pervasive fashionability of Post-Modernism cast many aspects of modernist design into a historical perspective, the 'Masters' range included the resurrection of furniture by Gunnar Asplund, Breuer, Frank Lloyd Wright, Gerrit Rietveld, and Charles Rennie Mackintosh.

Also important in the wider projection of companies has been their involvement in cultural activities, a sphere in which Olivetti has been particularly prominent. Exhibitions specifically centred upon the company's own products and designers were widely disseminated, typified by such shows as Olivetti: Form and Concept put on at the Musée des Arts Decoratifs in Paris (as well as at venues in Barcelona, Madrid, Edinburgh, London, and Tokyo between 1969 and 1971) and Os Artistas e a Olivetti (The Artists and Olivetti) shown in Sao Paolo, Brazil in 1976. However, wider aspects of Italian artistic heritage were celebrated in a series of art-historical films by Carlo Ludovico Ragghianti in the 195os, as well as in exhibitions such as the Italian Modern Art Exhibition 1910-35, drawn from the Mattioli Collection, which was shown in New York and Europe in the late 196os and early 1970s. Film and photography have also been a potent vehicle for the projection of company ethos, environment, design practice, and social policy, with commissions from, and about, other countries, cultures, ecology, and the environment. Even gifts, souvenirs, desk diaries, and company calendars, first introduced in 1951, were a part of the process of establishing the company's personality as cultural. Corporate gifts and souvenirs have included melamine paper knives by Marcello Nizzoli (1960), a melamine desk tidy by Bruno Munari (1960), a canvas bri case by Giorgio Soavi (1965), and a enamelled-iron pencil tray (196) and electric table clock (1968) by Ettore Sottsass Jr., many of which became extremely sought after as status symbols by business executives and managers.

## 'The New International Style'

Globalization of the designed object accelerated significantly in the 198os with the emergence of design groups such as Memphis. It originated in 1981 in Milan and, centred around Ettore Sottsass Jr. effectively set on a commercial footing many ideas which had originated in the studio-based experimentation of 197os Italy. Memphis furniture, lighting, ceramic, glass, and jewellery designs drew upon the symbols, forms, materials, and decorative motifs of mass culture; it utilized seemingly mundane materials such as plastic laminates, and emphasized surface decoration, colour, and layers of meaning at the expense of apparent function. Such products radically undermined both the architectonic principles of the modernist aesthetic inherent in international style furniture and the linear gracefulness that had generally characterized design-conscious Italian products since the Second World War. In addition to Sottsass, the group had a strong international range of contributors to its output, including Andrea Branzi, Michele De Lucchi, Matheo Thun, and Marco Zanini from Italy, Michael Graves and Peter Shire from the United States, Hans Hollein from Austria, Xavier Mariscal from Spain, and Shiro Kuramata and Arata Isozaki from Japan. During the 198os, shows of the group's work were held in the major museums in many capital cities around the world and its products marketed under the banner of the 'New International Style'.26 This notion of a New International Style' indi-

During the 1980s many associated 'designer' products were introduced to this sphere of domestic life. The Starck toothbrush, with its Brancusi-like sculptural form and conical 'pedestal' has become an icon of an emergent 'designer' culture.

![](images/4de3285d748e050f76704b6c51d4154f4d1676d5b5eaf9a9032f9380256e84b3.jpg)

cated the commodification of particular cultural concepts within a global context. Endowed with the immediate cultural status conferred through featuring widely in exhibitions and the more image-oriented media across the world, Memphis products were the preserve of an affluent élite who saw them essentially as works of art.

This notion of 'design as art', where design was generally collected for its visual identity and implicit cultural values rather than any premium on function or practicality [111], was also reinforced by the outlook of companies such as Officina Alessi²7 from Italy or the New York-based Swid Powell [112]. Specializing in what has been termed the tabletop industry' or 'micro-architecture', both companies focused on the commissioning of small-scale pieces of decorative art by well-known architects and designers, whose buildings, interiors, tastes, personalities, and exhibitions were increasingly widely covered in the media.

Officina Alessi launched a series of Tea and Coffe Piazzas²9 which were commissioned from eleven internationally recognized architects [113], including Robert Venturi, Michael Graves, and Charles Jencks from the United States, Alessandro Mendini and Paolo Portoghesi from Italy, Oscar Tusquets from Spain, and Hans Hollein from AuTv w cn9 priced individually at about £12,o0o, and galleries in Milan and New York simultaneously hosted their launch in 1983, reinforcing the cultural status and economic exclusivity of the objects.

Swid Powell was founded in 1982 by Nan Swid and Addie Powell, significantly both previous employees of Knoll International, the former as design director in product design development, the latter a sales vice-president. Swid Powel's first range of architect-designed pieces, including porcelain dinnerware, glassware, and silverplate, was launched in 1984 in six American cities, proving an instant commercial success in the upper echelons of the market-place. The company continued to maintain a fashionable aura throughout the decade, sustained by such high-profile events as the striking window displays in the Bergdorf Goodman department store which were planned to coincide with the American Institute of Architects' 1988 convention in New York. Many of the displays incorporated Swid Powell artefacts,

It is also clear when reviewing the names of many of the designers working for Memphis, Officina Alessi, and Swid Powell and other such enterprises that they and their designs have become global commodities just as have the more anonymous products of IBM, Philips, Sony, or Benetton.

# Design Promotion, Profession, and Management

The nineteenth and early twentieth centuries had seen the proliferation of many societies which sought to promote design as a significant activity, whether from a social, cultural, political, or economic point of view [114]. These included such diverse bodies as the Swedish Society of Industrial Design (Svenska Slödförinengen), which had been established in 1845 to encourage and promote handicraft in industry, the Finnish Society of Crafts and Design of 1879, the Art Workers' Guild and the Arts and Crafts Exhibition Society, founded in Britain in 1884 and 1888 respectively, the Hungarian Decorative Art Society (Magyar Iparmüvészeti Társulat) of 1885, the Polish Applied Arts Society of 1901, the Netherlands Association for Crafts and Industrial Art (Nederlandsche Vereeniging voor Ambachts en Nijverheidskunst), the Danish Society of Arts and Crafts and Industrial Design of 1907, and the National Alliance of Art and Industry in the United States of 1912. The more significant impact in Germany of the activities of the Deutscher Werkbund in the twenty-five years following its foundation in 19o7 has already been discussed in Chapters I and 2. Like other similar less influential bodies formed in its wake, such as the British Design and Industries Association (DIA) founded in 1915, it sought to achieve recognition of the economic significance of design in manufacturing industry and thus indirectly helped to enhance the professional status of the designer.

The long-term difficulties inherent in efforts to achieve recognition of the design profession in manufacturing industry can be seen to reside partially in the conflict between notions of the individual artistic personality and the economic and technological realities of manufacturing industry. Indeed, for much of the twentieth century there has been considerable reservation about designers in manufacturing circles. Several instances of such suspicion early in the century can be detected in the debates about design and standardization in industry. But there was also considerable disagreement amongst designers and their propagandists themselves, centering on opposition of individuality and standardization, subjectivity and objectivity, and artistic licence and social responsibility. Notable examples of such ideological differences already encountered include those between Henry van de

![](images/7e4cbcf6134e3d0af72cbe33d67556e324ce90092a9c3659b8dbba849b6873b5.jpg)

166 DESIGN PROMOTION, PROFESSION, AND MANAGEMENT

## 114 W. H. Gispen

The BKI (Dutch Association for Art in Industry) was   
established in 1924 in   
conjunction with the Instituui voor Sieren Nijverheidskunst (Institute of Decorative and Industrial Art—ISN) which had been established three years earlier. Somewhatélitst in character, with a high   
premium on aesthetics, the BKI largely failed to capture the imagination of

Velde and Hermann Muthesius at the Deutscher Werkbund before 1914 or between Wassily Kandinsky and Alexander Rodchenko, his wife Varvara Stepanova, and Vladimir Tatlin around 1920. Although these rifts have been accorded a high historical profile on account of the dominanceof personality-centreddesign-historical writing, resonances of such concerns were felt in many countries in the boardrooms of everyday manufacturing industry, where the spectre of the artistic personality, oblivious of the realities of production technology, consumer preferences and market strategies, haunted the thoughts of many industrialists and businessmen. In Britain, for example, the Council for Art and Industry's 1937 report on Design and the Designer in British Industry stated that a key 'difficulty which has to be overcome is the existence of a mutual distrust between artists and manufacturers'.1

Design-historical writing has often obscured much of the wider picture of the professional status of the designer and the role of design in industry through its general focus on successful partnerships between designers and industry: attention is drawn to Sweden, for example, where Wilhelm Kåge worked as Art Director for the ceramics manufacturer Gustavsberg, and Simon Gate and Edward Hald helped to establish the artistic reputation of the glass manufacturer Orrefors; to Italy, where Marcello Nizzoli designed for Olivetti and Gio Ponti for the ceramics manufacturer Richard-Ginori and the furniture maker Fontana; and to the United States, where the first generation of industrial designers such as Raymond Loewy, Henry Dreyfuss, and Walter Dorwin Teague worked for large, well-known corporate clients.

However, the widely-felt uncertainty of the connotations of terms commonly used in the interwar years such as 'commercial art' or 'graphic design', 'industrial art' or 'industrial design' reflected the inability of designers to establish a clearcut professional identity or status. Indeed, the use of such terms can lend insights into the changing politics of professional validation. For example, the Chartered Society of Designers (CSD) in London, the leading professional organization for designers in Britain, began life in 193o as the Society of Industrial Artists (SIA) and despite a radical reorganization at the end of the Second World War, did not actually incorporate the word 'design' into its title until 1965, when it became the Society of Industrial Artists and Designers. The last change of title, to Chartered Society of Designers, reflected the continuing drive for more widespread professional recognition by the business and manufacturing community: the omission of the word 'Artists', together with any notion of the idiosyncracies that such a term might still have been thought to imply, was perhaps as significant as the word 'Chartered', with all of its professional connotations.

## The design profession in interwar Britain

The Society of Artists (SIA) was established with two distinct aims, the first of which was

to establish the profession of the Designer on a sounder basis by forming a controlling authority to advance and protect the interests of all engaged in the production of Design for Industry, Publishing and Advertising. The second purpose is to assemble the resources of Design as a vital factor in British Industry and so to assist the advancement of British Trade, both at home and abroad.²

The founding of the SIA appeared to preage a furry of official design activity in Britain in succeeding years: a Board of Trade Committee under the Chairmanship of Lord Gorell was appointed in the following year, 1931, to report on Art and Industry;³ late in 1933 the Council for Art and Industry was established under the auspices of the Board of Trade, making its mark through the publication of a number of designcentred reports (including Design and the Designer of 1937), the organization of exhibitions, liaison with industry, and involvement in design education; in 1936 a National Register of Industrial Art Designers was established, and the Royal Society of Arts appointed ten members to act as a new select faculty of Designers for Industry (upgraded by the Home Office to Royal Designers for Industry (RDI) in the following year). It was also a decade which heralded a number of BBC radio programmes devoted to design matters, saw the publication of Herbert Read's celebrated pro-modernist text, Art and Industry (1934),4 and Nikolaus Pevsner's Pioneers of the Modern Movement (1936)5 and An Enquiry into Industrial Art in England (1937),6 as well as the mounting of a number of widely reported exhibitions7 including the large-scale 'British Art in Industry' show at the Royal Academy, with 2,223 exhibits on show. The Design and Industries Association (DIA) was also active at the time in its promotion of the design cause, publishing its own magazines, newsletters, books, and other design propaganda, as well as organizing public exhibitions. But, as has been suggested in 'Britain and Modernism' in Chapter 2, such apparently clear-cut evidence, though cited by many historians of British design,8 can be misleading in its portrayal of a real sea change in terms of recognition of the designer in industry. The long history of British design has often embraced the establishment of many official design-related committees, particularly in times of economic difficulty, when the government or its agencies wish to be seen to be working towards improving the position. An early instance of this was the Select Committee on Art and Manufactures which was appointed in 1836 in the light of worries about increasingly strong competition from foreign manufacturers. Almost 150 years later, in not dissimilar circumstances, and indicating in some ways how little the picture had really changed, the Department of Education and Science and the Design Council commissioned a report on The Industrial Design Requirements of Industry which came to the conclusion that foreign companies generally

value more highly the contribution made by design to market success, they spend more money on industrial design and give the designer considerably more say in the final selection of the design for manufacture. As a result they appear to attract and motivate designers who are generally very competent and highly creative.

The DIA has left a considerable legacy for historians in its Yearbooks, magazines, and the prolific writings of its key members. None the less, its influence should not be overestimated: its attitude to industrial design in the 192os was ambivalent, its membership was small by comparison with the Deutscher Werkbund (in 1930 Werkbund membership stood at 3,oo0 and the DIA's at 82o), and its magazines had an extremely difficult time in the difficult economic climate of the 1930s. Design in Industry launched in 1932 lasted for only two issues, the lifespan of Trend in Everyday Things of 1936 was equally brief, with only Design for Today lasting for any length of time, between 1933 and 1935. Similarly, although the state-funded Council for Art and Industry, and its predecessor the British Institute of Industrial Art (founded in 1920), may be researched easily through their published reports and the papers lodged in the Public Record Office at Kew, their contemporary impact is more difficult to evaluate. The Design and the Designer in Industry report of 1937, claimed by one British historian to be 'by far the most influential'0 of the Council for Art and Industry's publications, may well have been read in official circles, but the publisher, His Majesty's Stationery Office, was concerned a year after issue that only 1,594 of its 5,o00 print run had been purchased.

However, the interwar years saw the emergence of a small number of significant advertising agencies, such as S. H. Benson (established in 1893) and W. S. Crawford (established in 1914). Ashley Havinden joined Crawfords in the early 192os, becoming director of art and design in 1929, and did much to gain recognition for the firm as one of the leaders of the field in Britain. Multidisciplinary design consultancies along the lines of the American model, as discussed in Chapter 3, were slow to emerge in Britain. Early instances included the Bassett-Gray Group of Artists and Writers which became the Industrial Design Partnership (1935-40). The founders were Milner Gray, Misha Black, and Walter Landor: Gray and Black were significantly involved in the establishment of the Design Research Unit (DRU) in 1943, a consultancy aimed at providing the necessary design expertise for postwar industry; Landor founded the internationally renowned consultancy, Landor Associates, in San Francisco in 1941 after emigrating to the United States in the previous year. None the less the American with the opening of the London office of Raymond Loewy. This was launched in London in 1936 under the management of Carl Otto, who had worked at General Motors and with Bel Geddes, and employed Beresford Evans and Douglas Scott as designers. Before its closure in 1939 its clients included Allied Ironfounders, Electrolux, General Electric Company, and Rootes, the motor manufacturers.

## The design profession: fresh opportunities after 1945

The post-1945 period saw many significant shifts in design practice and approach: fresh attitudes were engendered by the implications of changing technologies, which were in turn stimulated by widespread European and American involvement in the Second World War; by an increasing reconsideration of the extent to which the methods and approaches adopted by designers in the interwar years were still appropriate or postwar needs; and by the growing perception in design circles that there was a fresh opportunity to establish in business circles a clearer perception of the importance, or professional status, of the designer than had generally been the case in the 192os and 1930s.11 There was a clear move away from faith in the primacy of intuition and individuality towards the recognition that the increasingly complex problems of an increasingly technologically-dependent society could only be solved by a coalition of a range of skills and expertise. Ironically, the individual celebrity status which a number of the emergent breed of industrial designers in the United States had sought to cultivate in the 193os, in order to gain both public recognition and commercial advantage, tended to reinforce doubts in business circles about individual designers' abilities to grasp the wider manufacturing, marketing, advertising, retailing, social, and economic implications of product development. The principles of teamwork upon which the larger American practices were in fact organized pointed towards the basis upon which many postwar consultancies were to be organized. This notion of teamwork had been strengthened by the wartime experience of many designers who worked alongside engineers and scientists in the war effort, gaining a more complete understanding of new materials and processes. Examples of this multidisciplinary approach may be seen in consultancies such as the Japanese GK Industrial Design Associates which, from its modest beginnings in 1957, has grown to embrace product design, communication design, architectural and environmental design, research, planning and development, and design studies; Pentagram, founded in London in 1972 from a range of different types of expertise capable of catering for the complex demands of large corporations which were placing an increasing premium on design, was similarly able to deal with a broad range of commissions. Other examples include Total Design, founded in the Netherlands in 1963, Minale Tattersfield, founded in

## State initiatives in design promotion

The potential of design as a means of gaining a market edge was acknowledged by a number of governments in their establishment of official design bodies to promote its value to industry and the public. The most significant of these was the Council of Industrial Design (COID) established in London in 1944 on an initial grant of £55,000, ready to assist in the anticipated postwar export drive. Its specific aims were to promote good design in industry; to disseminate design advice and information to manufacturers, government departments, and others; to organize design exhibitions and advise on design education and training; and to educate the public in the importance of good design. Amongst its most important early initiatives was the organization of the 1946 Britain Can Make It Exhibition. Providing a sweeping vista of the benefits that design could provide for the home, work, and leisure this large-scale exhibition in the Victoria and Albert Museum attracted 1,432,369 visitors and included a display designed by Misha Black of the DRU entitled 'What Industrial Design Means'. Centred on the problem of designing an egg-cup for mass-production, Black showed how the industrial designer consulted with management, engineers, and salesmen in order to determine such matters as proportion, practicality, colour, and decoration, and how he needed to have an understanding of materials and mass-production technology. As well as advising industry through its industrial officers and library and information service, the COID organized regional conferences and exhibitions, furnished show houses, commissioned reports, and published books and, from 1949 onwards, its own magazine, Design. It has undergone many changes of fortune since then, often due to wider economic problems which at some times have threatened its grant-in-aid from government and at others have enhanced its profile, as with the widely publicized design initiatives of the Conservative government under Mrs Thatcher in the 198os. The COID established a Design Centre in central London in 1956, an information centre and showroom for exhibitions of well-designed goods. During the 196os its centre of gravity shifted away from consumer goods towards engineering, and in 1972 it was renamed the Design Council in recognition of its aim 'to wed engineering and industrial design in one body dedicated to improving design throughout all sections of British industry'. In 1994 the Design Council was dramatically cut in size, resulting in the closure of the Design Centre and all regional offices, this leading to a complete re-appraisal of its role.12

Similar state-funded organizations were established in a number of I

Estetyki Producji (BNEP/Office for the Supervision of Aesthetic Production) was established in 1947 within the Ministry of Culture and Art under the directorship ofWanda Telakowska. Its aim was to invigorate the design profession in the immediate postwar years, to improve standards of design in industry through the provision of models and prototypes, and to promote greater aesthetic awareness in everyday life. However, its ambitions were soon thwarted by the Stalinist reorientation of the State of the late 194os.13 Later, in 1960, the Council of Design and Aesthetics of Industrial Production was established to promotindutrial desg activityalthoug i efectivepact was vey limited. Elsewhere in eastern Europe, state initiatives were equally problematic. In the USSR, VNIITE (the All-Union Scientific Research Institute for Technical Aesthetics) was set up under the directorship of Yuri Soloviev in 1962, but its subsequent achievements were considerably constrained by high levels ofofficial bureaucracy and planning. Itsgreatest strengths ay in ergonomics an nginer design.

In West Germany, the German Parliament voted in 1951 to establish a Rat für Formgebung (Design Council) which was inaugurated in 1953 to further the understanding of design in manufacture. Although it received attention in the Milan Triennali it was not highly effective in the 196os and 197os, but with support from the Ministry of Economic Affairs it has played a continuing role in promoting greater awareness of design in German industry, a task of considerably greater complexity in the wake of the unification of the FDR and GDR. In France, the picture of state support for design has been more ambivalent, although the state has provided architects and designers with many high-profile commissions which during the 198os included furniture at the Elysée Palace, furnishing of the French Embassy in Washington, the interior design of the Musée d'Orsay, and redecoration work at the Louvre and the Ministry of Finance. The Centre de Création Industrielle (CCI), first established in 1969, was funded by the Ministry of Culture from 1973 in order to promote the cultural dimensions of French design. In 1975 the Ministry of Industrial and Scientific Development set up the Conseil Superieur de la Création Esthétique Industrielle, to complement in economic terms the CCI's cultural role, but following widespread lack of interest and support, as well as antipathy to any notions of intervention from the industrial sector, was soon wound up.

Other European initiatives include the commissioning in 1960 by the Irish Government's Exports Board of a panel of Scandinavian design experts to report on potential design strategies that the Republic might adopt.15 Their deliberations may be seen to have resulted in the Government's establishment of the Kilkenny Design Workshops (KDW) in 1965. Following Scandinavian models the KDW sought to develop the design potential of crafts-based indus-

It was one of the first recipients of the G-Mark, an award for aesthetic excellence in design instituted in 1957 by the Design Promotion Council of the Japanese Ministry of Trade and Industry.

![](images/ab3bae80384fe6e99e406d7f085553bc7b8727b30c022af7459fedbfc915ffc0.jpg)

tries, moving into the field of engineering design in the 197os. In Scandinavia itself, although the Swedish government set up a Design Centre in Stockholm in 1984 and issued a White Paper on design three years later, the Danish Design Council and Design Centre, founded in 1977 and 1987 respectively, are perhaps the best known design promotional bodies. Although independent they are supported by the Ministry of Industry and work with it and the Ministry of Education and Research to develop design education policy guidelines.

In Japan there was also a modest investment by the state in the promotion of design. In the 195os JETRO (Japan Export Trade Organization) sent five or six students a year to study design abroad, principally to the United States, but with a number studying in Germany and Italy, a scheme which was jointly funded by state and industry. From 1956 onwards JETRO was also responsible for inviting American and European designers to participate in design seminars which lasted for between two and three weeks. In the first five years of their existence almost four-fifths of the 35 designers who participated were American, a clear indication of the commercial efficacy of American design practice. In 1957 the Design Promotion Council of the Ministry of Trade and Industry (MITI) initiated a Design Awards Scheme, known as the G-Mark, as a stimulus to industry [115, 116]. This was followed in 1959 by MITI's establishment of a Design Division, and in the following year the Japan Design House was set up as a permanent information centre for well-designed Japanese prod-

## 116 GK Design

Soy sauce bottle for Kikkoman Corporation, Japan, late 1950s This design did much to establish the reputation of the GK Design consultancy as well as the Kikkoman company which grew rapidly from its origins as a family firm to a large national company. Although the elegant form of the bottle in many ways conforms to an international modernist aesthetic it also marks an effective design solution to the marketing of soy sauce in a table-top container, much in the manner of the ubiquitous tomato ketchup ott nUSAy Kikkoman was extremely sceptical that this would be effective in a market-place dominated by tradition, but it became a market leader.

![](images/2fe6609726fedad7abba5cd8bad9b828303cff26926c13050938904331bc5c63.jpg)

ucts. This had been inspired both by the COID's London Design Centre and the Den Permanente (established in 1931) exhibitions of Danish Design, which received international prominence in 1958 through its receipt of the Italian Compasso d'Oro award. Finally, in 1969 the Japan Industrial Design Promotion Organization (JIDPO) was established along the lines of the British Council of Industrial Design. From 1975 it also became responsible for the Design Development Programme for regional industries, funded both by national and regional governments, which laid particular stress on design as a management resource. A further initiative was the establishment in 1981 of the Japan Design Foundation (JDF), funded by national and local government as well as commerce and industry.

Under the directorship of Kazuo Kimura, it aimed to promote Japanese design to the forefront of the international stage. It organized the Biennial Design Festival and Competition at Osaka, the first of which was held in 1983 and attracted considerable attention through the granting of the First International Honorary Award for the Encouragement of Design Activities to the British Prime Minister, Margaret Thatcher. More recently, in 1992, the International Design Centre Nagoya Inc. was established with a capital of \$100 million as a quasi-governmental corporation with funding from national, regional, and local government as well as the business sector. The largest centre of its kind in Japan, its aims were to bring together community, industry, and the design profession, establishing an R & D centre, a design resource centre, and a design museum with a shopping mart open to the profession, industry, and the public.

Elsewhere in the Far East, Korea has a design promotion policy dating back to 1966, although the government's investment has been more heavily directed towards design education and exhibitions, bringing in educationalists from Japan and West Germany and funding overseas training and study tours. In 197o the Korea Design and Packaging Center (DKPC) was established and a GD mark for 'good design' instituted in 1986, although it was largely funded by industry. The Taiwan Design Promotion Centre was founded in 1979 to raise the levels of industrial design in Taiwan and to improve perceptions of its products abroad, much of its budget deriving from the Ministry of Economic Affairs. In Singapore, the Singapore Trade Development Board has included design promotion amongst its activities since 1984 and, following the British Design Council's model of the early 1980s, paid for up to 75 per cent of a design consultancy's fees for firms using such expertise for the first time.

## Professional design organizations

From the 194os onwards there has been a significant increase in the number of design organizations, reflecting the growing number of professionals in the field as well as their perceived need to campaign collectively for greater recognition of their value to business, commerce, and society.

In the United States the Society of Industrial Designers was established in New York in 1944, initially with fourteen members (including Loewy, Bel Geddes, Teague, Deskey, Van Doren, and Russel Wright) but growing to almost 100 members by 1949. It aimed 'to improve the vocation of industrial design, by providing the mechanism through which the members of the profession can agree and take action on every type of common problem' as well as to promote better standards of industrial design education and to improve public and industrial understanding of their work. In order to provide a showcase for many examples of their members' work from 1949 onwards it produced an annual publication entitled US Industrial Design. In 1955 the organization changed its name to the American Society of Industrial Designers, merging in 1965 with two other bodies to become the Industrial Designers Society of America (IDSA). According to an article in the American business magazine Fortune,16 by the late 1950s there were about 30o design consultancies in the United States, a fact that also reflected the growing number of in-house design departments in manufacturing industry. Many of them increasingly moved into the broader arena of design management rather than dealing with products or graphic design in isolation. Many of these changes can be followed in the pages of Industrial Design which, founded in 1954, was the leading magazine for design professionals in the United States; in 1980 it was retitled IDMagazine Industrial Design.

In Italy in the 195os the names of designers were important selling tools, but their role in industry was generally much more concerned with product aesthetics than with the wider concerns occupying American and, to an increasing extent, British consultancies. In 1956 the Italian Association for Industrial Designers (ADI) was founded and many of their concerns can be discerned in the magazine Stile Industria which was launched in 1954 and edited by Alberto Roselli. Published in Italian and English, it was an important vehicle for growih a, although it closed in the difficult economic climate of 1963.

In 1951 the Japan Advertising Artists Club was founded, followed by the Japanese Industrial Designers Association (JIDA) which was founded in the following year with 25 members. It has grown considerably since then, in tandem with the growing recognition of design in manufacturing industry discussed in Chapter 5, with 6o1 full members in 1985 (284 being employed by companies, 258 being freelance, and 59 drawn from education). It also published its own magazine Industrial Design, which by 1985 had about 3,000 subscribers, although there were only about twenty subscribing from abroad.

Throughout the 1950s and 1960s many other national design associations were formed17 and there was an increased awareness of the wider international climate, stimulated by the growing number of professional design magazines and the holding of international conferences, as at Aspen, Colorado, from the early 195os. The International Council of Societies of Industrial Design (ICSID) was established in 1957, mounting conferences biennially at various locations around the world, followed in 1963 by the International Council of Graphic Design Associations (ICOGRADA). Designers of many nationalities increasingly shared many concerns, and in 1981 ICOGRADA, ICSID and IFI (the International Federation of Interior Designers) held a joint conference in Helsinki, examining design issues on a world scale.

# A rational approach to design: the Hochschule für Gestaltung at Ulm: 1953-1969

Penny Sparke suggested in her book Consultant Design:

While Germany sells design in the name of science, Italy in the name of art, Scandinavia in the name of craft and the USA in the name of business, all these national images of design were necessary strategies in the highly competitive markets of the immediate post war years. The role of the designer was to help develop a marketing strategy which would give his product a special place in that market.18

The scientific, rational, and efficient outlook associated with German design in the later 195os, as typified in the austere aesthetic of Braun products of the period, was very much bound up with the innovative curriculum at the Hochschule für Gestaltung at Ulm (HfG).

Inge Scholl, whose brother and sister had been executed by the Nazis in 1943, wanted to establish a new international school of design as part of an express commitment to social and moral reconstruction in the postwar years. In fact many German universities had shown considerable continuity in personnel and outlook with the prewar years, and so the envisaged school was intended to reflect a distinctive alternative personality. Scholl established a foundation in 195o in order to bring the school into being, and funding was also secured from the US government, German federal and regional government, and the business sector in the Federal Republic. Opening its doors in 1953 as a private institution, and thus free from the constraints of the local and national education system, its original impetus was focused on the idea of a new Bauhaus: visiting teachers included Johannes Itten, Josef Albers, and Mies van der Rohe, all of whom had contributed to the original Bauhaus ethos in the 1920s. In 1955 the HfG moved into new buildings designed by Max Bill, who had been installed as director in the previous year. Students and staff were involved in their construction and furnishing, much as the Bauhaus students had been under Gropius at Dessau in the mid-1920s.

However, within a short space of time ideological differences between Bill and several of his younger colleagues began to emerge. These centred on their strong desire to emphasize the theoretical dimensions of the curriculum at the expense of the studio- and workshop-based studies upon which Bill placed a much higher priority. He resigned as director in 1956, and the running of the school was placed in the hands of a rectoral board of five (later three) members, renewable on an annual basis. From then there was an increasing emphasis on science and other disciplines which cast a fresh perspective on design thinking and practice, leading to Bills resignation from the school in 1957. His departure was also exacerbated by personal as well as curricular conflicts with Tomás Maldonado who had joined the staff in 1956.

Maldonado played a key role in theoretical debates, particularly through his many contributions to, and editorship of, the HfG's magazine Ulm, which was published between 1958 and 1959 and from 1962 to 1968. Other HfG theorists included Claude Schnaidt and Giu Bonsiepe who, following the closure of the school, became an important figure in South American design.

Richard Hamilton, who had visited the HfGin 1959, put into words the distinctiveness of the institution thus:

The major difference between the HfG and any other school of design in the world is precisely in the rejection of those principles which gave the Bauhaus its meaning and almost every school now available for the design student.19

By the later 195os mathematical, statistical, and analytical methods, together with sociology, anthropology, physical and behavioural psychology, and twentieth-century cultural history, were considered sufficiently important elements to rank alongside the more practical aspects of the curriculum. In the early 196os methodology and theory assumed a dominant role in many areas of work, creating further tensions with those who placed a greater priority on the practical elements. This state of relative ideological confusion led to the creation of a new constitution for the school in 1962, when once more its direction fell under the control of a single person, initially Otl Aicher. It was also in this period that the radicalism of the institution came under attack in the press, accompanied by a funding crisis. The regional government served the school with an ultimatum to amend its constitution in 1963, which it duly did, and further political problems were averted until 1967 when there was renewed pressure to bring the school into

## 117 Hans Gugelot et al.

M125 Furniture system, 1957 Gugelot began to explore furniture systems for the Wohnbedarf company in Zurich in 1950, before working for the Braun electrical appliance company. He was appointed professor at the Hochschule für Gestaltung at Ulm in 1956, and the rational, systematized approach to furniture seen here (without doors) reflected the scientific approach to design increasingly adopted in the curriculum. The M125 range was put into large-scale production by the Wilhelm Bofinger company.
